RATING: 7.5
Placement game with multiple ways to score points, with an interesting game mechanic (dice options), a short duration (3 rounds), and good scalability (2-4).

Gameplay consists of rolling a set of dice, allocating these dice to the appropriate actions, which are now available to select for that turn, and then selecting and performing an action in turn order.  An action can be: a)place cubes in the appropriate neighbourhood, which generally results in area majority points at the end of a round, b)select a card, which generally gives a fairly significant potential benefit to the player at some point in the game, c)select gold, which is used for building, moving the supervisor, and buying extra dice, d)select camels, which are used to build, or defend against the supervisor, e)move the supervisor, which results in moving your own and/or other players cube(s) out of neighbourhoods and into the caravan (this can be good or bad depending).

Instead of selecting one of these actions (or in addition to, if not playing the 2-player game, which works very well by the way), a player can build a building, paying generally camels and gold.  This is beneficial because the building gives the player special powers for the rest of the game, and eventually leads to a method of scoring points, provided enough buildings are built.

The caravan provides small initial points, but is good for longer-term accumulation of points, as the more goods that are placed (assuming they are placed in different levels of the caravan), the more points the player scores, and the caravan can potentially score more than just once per round.  Overall, there are the following ways to score in this game: 1)placement in souks (neighbourhoods), 2)caravan, 3)buildings, and 4)special cards.

Although this is a light and relatively short game, it does require some planning and strategy and there is definite player interaction from the action selection and the supervisor.  Also, each game can play quite differently depending on the number of players, and the different strategies employed.

Own the [boardgame=149959]Souks[/boardgame] mini-expansion from the [boardgame=47156]Ystari Box[/boardgame] expansion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
While the mechanics are great and the components and boards are beautiful (the main reason I bought the game), this wouldn't be nearly as good if it weren't such a tight game. You never can do everything you want to (in fact far less), despite the fact that you're guarenteed 21 turns. I find it a very relaxing while still exciting, and there's nothing like chucking 12 dice!

UPDATE: I had my first 4-player game, and must say the game suffered from the extra player. It still works, but on many turns you'll be taking poor actions or cards. On the other hand, a caravan strategy becomes much more viable (since the pips on the die control the supervisor rather than the number of dice), and the building that let's you move the supervisor is suddenly pretty powerful.

RATING: 7
Another fun economic development game. A supervisor is moved somewhat like Caylus' Provost but the result is a cube that could be sent to the point accumulating caravan. Cards can be very important to development. Actions are driven by dice roll that are allocate to: highest to gold and then lowest #s allocated from lowest to highest. This could leave out one or two market sectors. # of dice can mean: # of gold, # of camels, # of cubes to a souk, or (the next two are always choices) grab a card or move the supervisor. 3 weeks in a game. 7 days (turns) in a week.

RATING: 9
Impressions after 2 3 player games:

Feels a lot like Puerto Rico but with less... that is, about 50% less play-time, 50% less learning curve and maybe 20-25% less strategical/tactical depth. 

That's actually a really good deal, so I think this game should appeal strongly to anybody who enjoys euros. 

The only down-side is that the game is largely an incremental one - you don't get the thrill of setting up and excecuting a "big move"... I mean, maybe you got really lucky with the dice, but that just doesn't give the same kind of satisfaction. This is where it loses out to games like Samurai, though, conversely, that game cannot offer the different paths to victory that Ysphahan does. 

Overall, a great game. We'll see how well it holds up over time. 

Edit:

Game going up to a 9 because so many people enjoy it so quickly. Also, I played a game with 4 and it may have been even better than with 3, 2 on the other hand was not so hot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
*Rating based on one play.

This is a straight-forward game with an interesting dice mechanic.  The current player rolls a set of dice, and then players take turns picking from the resulting dice values.  The dice provide resources, money, or cubes that players place on a central board to score points.  There's a fair amount of routes that can be taken to victory.  It was an enjoyable play, but I do wonder how much replayability it has since it does not seem especially deep.

+Interesting dice drafting mechanic
+Mostly intuitive rules
+Plays quick with little down time
+Neat little wooden camel bits

-Limited number of cards and non-variable player boards could limit replayability
-The "Supervisor" pawn rules were a bit unintuitive
-Rather unusual color scheme using pastels

RATING: 7
2-4(3,4)

At a seven right now. It's fast, pretty light, and uses dice in a way that reduces the luck of the roll. The supervisor is an interactive element in stocking the caravan or kicking out of a souk. Camels and money can get pretty tight and are pretty crucial (money, especially, if you're trying to stock the Vase neighborhood and NEED all six values to roll). There are a lot of things to consider (souks, buildings, the caravan ... as well as trying to stay on top of resources) to keep things lively, but I'm not sure how long this one will remain interesting.

RATING: 8
{2 plays, 4 players} It might drop a tad down the line, but I'm really loving it right now.  Just enough decisions to make it fun, but not too many to slow things down.  The "caravan" strategy can be powerful, but so can the "souk  2" strategy.  Neither is a lock... nor a given, since you're somewhat at the mercy of the dice.  And can I just say that the dice allocating system is a brilliant piece of design.  Someone called this the current Web of Power, and that seems fitting.  The mechanics of each game are nothing alike, but the *feel* of each is the same for me: packs a LOT of game into 30-45 minutes, while still being fun and light.

RATING: 7
This is an interesting and very quick game that will likely be much lighter than expected.   The dice factor is important, but it is not a bad thing in the game since it only drives what can be done - not what must be done.  Also, everyone is subject to it equally so it can be planned for and managed.  There are a couple of approaches to take and depending on how the game develops, might lead you to a win.  This is good game that is light enough for casual players and families alike.   Great filler / opener / closer for a game night.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[i]Posted: 8/7/11, Rated: 8[/i]
{2P: ???, 3P: 8, 4P: ???}

[i]Yspahan[/i] has been an unplayed game in my collection for over a year now.  When I'm looking to teach a new game to my family, I usually sort on Board Game Rank.  Finally [i]Yspahan[/i] rose to the top, and we got it to the table.  I remember hearing a lot of buzz about the game, and was interested to see what it was like.

To my surprise, there were dice!  I'm always excited to see dice in a "Eurogame," because typically they represent too much chaos for that fare.  The extended result is that when you do find them, their contribution has been implemented in an interesting way.  In [i]Yspahan[/i], the dice control the access to resources or locations.  I enjoyed this mechanic better than the resources in [i][thing=55670]Macao[/thing][/i], but not as much as the risk mitigation [i][thing=34635]Stone Age[/thing][/i].

The ability to invest in special powers may have been interesting in 2006, but it is pretty commonplace in 2011.  This is another byproduct of the "cult of the new:" if you're not playing a game close to its release, then any innovations it may have introduced (and I'm not saying that [i]Yspahan[/i] innovated at all) will have had more time to be copied.

With three players, it's hard to find a reason to mess with your opponents versus optimizing your own play.  My understanding may change as we further explore this game.  I loved the camel meeples, and the artwork is richly saturated and pleasing.  [i]Yspahan[/i] can be a fast playing game, with just enough complexity to be intriguing, but not so much as to cause analysis paralysis.  In other words, it's a satisfying game to play with the family, and that's always a plus.

[url=http://www.boardgameswithscott.com/?p=65]BGWS Review[/url]

RATING: 5
The bucket of dice that comes with the game should be sufficient clue that this game has a significant random element. Whilst the dice aren't used in a roll and move or combative sense, the allocation to actions can fall favourably to a player or cut him off at the knees.

An escalating play that makes bash the leader inevitable and weakens the player who wields the axe. Prone to screwage with no real way to defend against such action, this game has the veneer of a structured engine building mechanism, but fails owing to the lottery of the dice and the lack of cohesive control.

Fine for people who don't mind the game playing them, but frustrating as hell for players who dislike chaos.

RATING: 7.75
I'd been wanting to play this game for a while, and when I finally did, it didn't disappoint. I played with 3 players, and it was really fun. It's not that long (less than 45 minutes), is fast-paced, and offers some interesting decisions, making it a perfect meaty filler. The game is balanced and offers different ways to score points. Even though the dice obviously add an element of luck, it's how you fit the dice results into your overall strategy that differentiates the good players from the bad. The player interaction is subtle and takes the form of choosing a certain group of dice to block someone or moving the supervisor to break up someone's souk, even though that might still net them some points. I've since played with 4 players, and it works just as well as with 3. The official 2-player variant on the Ystari site works, as well. There are a few minor rules changes, but it felt just the same as playing with more players--an excellent 2-player game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
At last a fresh game mechanism. (I won't say original since there is sure to be a precedent but I can't recall it) Played this several times now and no closer to establishing a viable strategy, have seen several approaches win. Fun, fast and challenging, what more can you want?
Update: After many more plays this seems to have the St. Petersburg problem. One dominant strategy means that everyone has to jump onboard and the game reduces to luck. But getting to that point is such great fun that I still highly recommend it.

RATING: 5
Played once. A co-player described this as "Caylus light." That's not a bad off-handed comparison. Players collect points by building buildings with camels and gold (I don't get it either); putting cubes on city quarters to claim control of them; and using a "supervisor" to ship cubes off in a caravan. Actions and available resources that the players may take are governed in some degree by rolling a clutch of dice. This is mitigated by allowing players to perform a range of actions regardless of the throw. The fun is in mini-maxing the process to earn more points than the next guy. More fun can be had by using the supervisor to interfere with the plans of others. Whee.

Although it can be quite important what the dice do on your turn, since you have first dibs then. Then again, which bonus cards you get can also impact your succes a lot. Very enjoyable gameplay combination between action choosing and push your luck. It's a nice filler and also pretty good as a gateway game.

Note: In my experience it depends heavily on group thinking whether the camel track gets used at all. In many games it feels like an appendix.

RATING: 4
Okay, I have played this game about 10 times now. The rules are pretty simple and even my 7 year old can play this game. However, there are many ways to score points and sometimes that is hard to see for a 7 year old. There is some luck involved but not too much. The nice thing is that it is a great family game that can be played in about 45 minutes, the two player rules (on ystari's website) work pretty well. I think my wife summed it up the other day with saying: "I can play this game and it doesn't hurt my brain while I am playing it".

++Update. I have about 20 games in now and I have come to the conclusion that the game is indeed broken. I made it to the semi finals at WBC and everybody pretty much plays the same strategy. The camel track. Get the houses in the middle that are opposite of each other, get the caravansarei and just deliver and get two cards every turn. It really seems there is only one way to win.

Lowered from a 7 to a 4.

RATING: 7
1 play w/ 4.  Light, easy to teach, and plays quickly.  A very nice gateway / social game.  Substantial luck - a late game roll with 3 dice in the vase district pretty much decided our game, since it netted the start player 20 points (with the last 2 buildings built).  But it was fun, and will likely see many more plays.

Update: This one has been hard to get back to the table, and I finally traded it away.  It is a little to heavy for non-gamers, and way too light for gamers.  It has lots of decisions to be made, but can be decided by the dice alone.  Perhaps the most frustrating part of it is that the dice won't necessarily decide the game - they will sometimes even out over the course of the game.  But it is just as likely that they will favor one person over the others, or decide the game with a key roll.  So you have to invest yourself into the game with the understanding that good play might win the game, or the dice might decide to have their way with you.  

Traded away

RATING: 7.5
Comprado a mila-spiele.de en medido conjunto en julio del 2010.
Consiste en hacer más puntos que los demás colocando cubos en los zocos de cuatro barrios diferentes, enviando mercancías a la caravana o construyendo edificios.  Me gusta mucho y me parece bastante origian el sistema de configuración de acciones disponibles (fase de suministros) a traves de la colocación de dados.
La partida dura 21 turnos repartidos en tres semanas. Al final de cada semana hay un recuento según los barrios ocupados y se limpia de nuevo el tablero. 
Las partidas dan la impresión de ser bastante rapidas... La unica pega es que es solo para 3 o 4 jugadores.

Vendido en Octubre 2014 a moteuchi en la bsk por 20€

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Yspahan fills that sweet spot that we were hoping Amyitis would, but didn't.  There is a lot of depth here, with just the right amount of luck to manage, all in a playing time that is quite reasonable (30-45 min).  By and large, the game is an economic area controller - roll the dice to determine possible (locale and number of) cube placements, trying to hold complete sets of colored buildings in various neighborhoods.  This is certainly the main road to success, but there's also a caravan that serves as a spoiler - you can take goods out of some shops and place them in the caravan for immediate smaller points, which, as you fill the caravan, become much larger points.  Thus, if one person dominates the caravan, it can be a game changer, but if both players are matched and cagey, filling the caravan equally and competing for souks, the game will come down to who managed tactics the best.  The cards and buildings allow more variation to come into play, mixing it up by allowing resource conversions to VPs, adding freebies to the boards, or gaining extra VPs or dice.  All in all, it's great to have a few strategic choices that are fairly clear (thus competitive) with many tactical options.  And the turns are quite short, tense, the game really flows along.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love dice euros, and I own quite a few now. This is definitely one of my favorites. You always have plenty of options, and the camels/supervisor allow for some interesting decisions: you can try to move the supervisor away from your opponents to score more points in the caravan, or you can force him upon them to make them give up spots on the board. The result is LOTS of indirect player interaction. The game also works well with two players (though I'd like to try a 2-p variant with each player using two colors to limit the board options).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
UPDATE: Second play was more promising, so I've bumped my rating up to a 5. The game is still soulless, and the choices aren't that interesting usually, but it at least moves along pretty quickly and every so often it throws you an interesting dilemma. A long way from a buy for me, or even from having much desire to play again, but if you asked me to play an Ystari game this is probably the one I'd take. At least it's short.
---
This is not bad actually, and of all the Ystari games perhaps the most appealing to  me; but that's just not saying too much. Yspahan is clearly trying to be sort of a "downmarket" Goa, minus the auctions, plus a Bailiff, and I think it's partially successful (it's no longer the purgatory of boring analysis that their previous games were). But there is also some weird stuff in there too - the most dismaying for me was the rule where the player order on the last turn is dictated by score at that time. But, getting an early pick in turn order is a big deal, and at that point there are huge numbers of points untallied, so our game saw the player who was *clearly* ahead get the powerful bonus first turn while *clearly* behind players got screwed. It was particularly irritating for me because I found out about this only as it came up. This is just dumb; there had to be a better way.

Yspahan was OK to play once, but really I wasn't very impressed by it. The buildings and action cards are a fairly transparent Goa ripoff (I'm not fundamentally against borrowing ideas, but I prefer if designers at least tweak things a *little* bit). I like dice games generally, but I'm not clear what they really accomplish in this case other than to occasionally make the game feel arbitrary. And ultimately, I strongly suspect that like Caylus this will be a "one way to win" game, and he whose dice line up most efficiently with that way to win will, well, win. There isn't enough player interaction in the game for it to work out any other way, really.

This is all problematic enough, and the real deal-killer then is that Yspahan has no theme. It's just another abstract building/placement game with something pasted on top of it. I don't demand awesome theme in all my games, but if a game has any complexity at all I do tend to expect some reasonable attempt. Yspahan does not deliver this - if it's possible, Yspahan feels even more thematically disjointed than Ystari's other games - so I doubt I'll play again.

Oh, one last thing, at $50 Yspahan is rather overpriced. Even if we assume I'm wrong and this is a nice little medium-weight, I don't think ti could justify the $50 pricetag.

RATING: 8
I went into this with pretty high hopes and am pleased to say Yspahan did not disappoint. This is a such a gem. Lots of different options on each turn, and some tense decisions since you only ever get to do so much and you always want to do so much more. The dice add the perfect amount of chance to the game... ensuring that nothing is 100% safe, thus forcing you to incorporate playing the odds into your strategy. Add in a good amount of interaction and multiple (seemingly) equally-valid ways to score and you've got a winner. Best of all, the game moves at a really brisk pace and packs a lot of punch into about 45 minutes. I'm happy to see that the two player variant is splendid, too. NEED to play this one more, as I can see my rating going even higher.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Ystari is a strange company. I was actively checking out their releases after Caylus, but they have yet to replicate that success for me, mainly because everything else they have released is either stripped down to the point of austerity or features irksome random or hidden elements. 

Yspahan is another lighter game in the vein of Mykerinos and Ys. A short game (about an hour) but there are some tough decisions throughout. There are 4 districts on the small board, and two major currencies: camels and coins. Players roll 9-12 dice at the start of their turn. Dice are then placed on the action track and essentially dictate how many camels/coins/cubes you get. Having a lot of dice in one area is very good (and is obviously out of control for the most part, you can tilt the odds but if you have consistently poor luck (me) then you can still often end up screwed), because you get to place more stuff or receive more currency. There are also two sub-aspects to the game. One is the building board, a track that allows you to improve your actions in 6 different ways, but which requires camel and gold tithes. Buildings also give you victory points. The other aspect is the caravan, which allows you to score points by sacrificing cubes off the board or camels to ship goods with larger quantities of cubes total in that area creating larger scores.

The sum of this game is a tight little affair. The dice can hand out some nice stuff to people at times, but I don't think the randomness is [i]that[/i] much of an issue in a lighter, faster game like this. That said, I also don't have any real desire to play this either. It works, the use of dice isn't all that offensive, but the decisions aren't good enough to make me want to sit down to it either. A pretty textbook 5 for me.

RATING: 8
Players compete in a fictional town somewhere near Yspahan to have their goods transported to Yspahan. They do this by setting up shop in various districts, and then have the overseer pick their goods to be sent off. The game features a novel dice rolling mechanism which works rather well, and overall has a very friendly feeling to it. The game's long term strategies are deceptive, as is illustrated by the fact that many people don't utilise the caravan to score: if used properly, it can be devastating. Nevertheless, the presence of the dice means that some lucky throws can end the game prematurely. I've not seen this happen very often, but the way the selection mechanic works *does* lead to a somewhat constrained feel in the 4P-game. I like the 2P-game better because of the ability to do 2 mostly good actions instead of just 1 average to below-average one. Nevertheless, a fun game, doesn't take too long, and doesn't feel artificially complex either. 

However, where the game really shines is the masterful way in which all the mechanics have been balanced. No single mechanic results in stronger strategies and tactics than another, and for every disadvantage there is an advantage. The overall amount of strategies may be limited, but seeing them come together as effortlessly as they do lends the game a feel-good aura I haven't experienced in quite a while. I've increased my rating because of this.

After Mykerinos my favourite from Ystari; and a proud member of the Dice Done Right Club™.

[b]Update[/b] You know what the one thing is that gets up my nose when playing Yspahan? It's not the caravan strategy: it's difficult to pull off, and easily derailed given any competent set of opponents. No: it's blatant dice luck in largely to completely filling up the Vase district in a single throw (two at most) with a few camels to defend against overseer movement. The amount of points thus obtained is very difficult to defend against. Especially with just 3 players there are too few actions in between dice selections to deal with this event.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This was pretty interesting: a nice, simple, and quick dice game with a couple of interesting choices to do with the dice.  The central board, and the roving supervisor, look to provide some element of interaction and a choice between filling shops or filling caravans (while at the same time preventing opponents from filling souks by shipping their goods).  However, camels seem to stop even this minimal level of interaction as they can protect goods in your shops.

The end result is something akin to a roll-and-write game, with little interaction between players outside of choosing dice piles.  It's also in danger of having obvious choices: if you roll 3+ dice in one section, it is hard not to pick them.  

There just doesn't seem to be the space available to carve out a unique strategic position.  That would be fine, if there was more interaction on the board to make up for it (compare with Concordia, Quadropolis, or Ginkgopolis, which have LOTS of interaction on the board to make up for somewhat limited strategic options).

I just don't see playing this over Dice Stars or Castles of Burgundy, let alone Grand Austria Hotel (which I haven't even played yet).

Maybe this is just groupthink, but regardless of what we ended up doing, the game does feel like the kind of game that can easily be "solved" (in the sense of Puerto Rico, where there are optimal strategies to adopt).  I'd be happy to play it again, but it can't survive against the competition.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players set up markets in Yspahan.  Players start a round of play be rolling dice and assigning them in order to game actions.  Actions are to take coins/camels, set up markets on the board in the city, move the "supervisor", or the "bad luck, nothing to do" take a card action.  Points are scored for caravaning (an offensive and defensive tactic), building, as well as cornering the market via property in the city.  Very nice game that is easy to learn, and a great example of how a bunch of dice can work in a game.  Fits right into that 1 hour sweet spot, too.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I appreciate the fact that, like in Hacienda, there's no majority-winner mechanism in the whole game: either you complete a souk in time to score it, or you don't.  There isn't anything that can be taken away from you; it's even pretty difficult for others to block you.  This means for the most part you're just playing your own game, but I like that.  What I find a little disappointing is that the scoring is very incremental, and your choices, especially near the endgame, often come down to just doing some calculations to figure out which one is worth 2 or 3 more points than the others.  It also seems like the first player in each day chooses camels about 80% of the time, which seems unbalanced, but maybe that's just groupthink.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.25

Both of these elements can be deal breakers for me (and have been in other games), but with this game they work together to create a very enjoyable experience. If the game was longer or if the dice weren't mitigated, the game would be unpleasant. Fortunately, the game plays crazy fast and there's always a competitive, positve move to be made with the dice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Only two plays of this one, and it's just OK. As far as dice strategy games, I prefer Troyes and then Castles of Burgundy. This one doesn't hook me the way Ystari games usually do because there just aren't enough interesting things to do with your dice. Your roll will dictate your action choice 99% of the time.

There is a bit of strategy in how you hit the neighborhoods vs the camel track. I thought the camel track was overpowered, as one player had the ability to get bonus cards every time he placed on the camel track. At one point, he played like 5 cards in a row to do a lot of stuff. Seemed like the obvious track to follow each game and that the other paths to victory and buildings are simply relatively underpowered. Yeah, gotta do the camel track. And then another player won with a basic neighborhood strategy whereby she spent her turns placing cubes in the souks and racking up points while hitting the camel track just a bit. 

So, at least the routes are balanced. Just wish the decisions were more interesting.

--Update--
I considered raising this from 8 to 9 but decided against it. However let me emphasize that this is a very solid 8, just not a game that "I want to play all the time"
Also, "simple rules" is probably not true. After explaining the game to more people, I find there are several minor (yet important) options on a player turn which can be hard to remember. Funny thing is, I think these small options are part of what give the game its charm; I feel very efficient (satisfying feeling for me) when I discard an unneeded card for an additional vase for example.

RATING: 5
While Caylus and Ys packed a lot of fun and challenge, we found Yspahan to be lacklustre and uninspiring.  The action/dice mechanic is definitely a new design element which works really well.  The problem here is with the lack of tension in the choices to make.  We ran out of all our cubes, camels, gold and cards.  Since the rules do not state that the stocks are infinite, we simply hoarded our ressources and drove up the scoreboard to a silly amount.  The buildings are easy to build and once all are built, the remaining actions are spent moving the supervisor around to fill up the caravan as many times as possible.  There is always the possibility that we didn't do something right.  But we looked at the FAQ, clarifications and session reports, and apparently other players had the same issue.  I'll keep looking but I'm not sure that even if we sort it out, that the game underneath grabs me all that much.  Other games of ressource management, area-control abound by the bucket.  I'ld rather play one that doesn't make me feel like I'm missing something.

RATING: 5
Neat mechanics and smooth, quick gameplay.  That's the good part.  Unfortunately, this game has the worst run-away leader problem I've ever seen in a game.  Some people respond, "But all the games I've been in have been close."  Great -- lucky you.  If, however, someone does manage to grab a substantial lead early, say through some fortuitous die rolls, there is no mechanism for catching them.  I don't particularly like games that have automatic 'screw-the-leader' mechanisms built-in, but they need to at least have a mechanism so THE OTHER PLAYERS CAN TRY TO DISRUPT THE LEADER.  In this game, you just have to sit back and watch... and fight over 2nd place. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Yspahan is a dice game with lots of different ways to score. The use of dice as an action selection mechanism is pretty neat, and in most cases there's something worthwhile to do for everyone. With bad rolls, however, like in most dice games, your options quickly start to disintegrate. Now, even taking a card (which you can always do) is not such a bad move but if it happens too often it induces the feeling of being played rather than doing anything meaningful yourself. The probability of being hit by bad rolls increases with the number of players, though, so less is probably better in this case.

RATING: 7
Yspahan is a light dice game that has an interesting mash-up of familiar game mechanics. It's one of those games where dice decide what you can do and you have to make the best of it, and I like those sorts of games. I can see many people enjoying this. And throwing a handful of dice is always fun! I'm surprised how much of a benefit the cards can give you - but that's the point of the game: you can't let anyone capitalize on anything or they will dominate. Everyone has to do their best to react properly and capitalize when they can. I think this game is deeper than it appears to be on the surface.

RATING: 5
My first few plays of this game were wonderful experiences.  Yspahan offered a lot of fun decisions, with some original mechanics meshed with familiar ones to make an interesting light-medium weight abstract/area-majorities game.  My initial rating of 8 reflected this.  However, after my subsequent plays I find yspahan doesn't scale well and is really only a game for three players, as the limitation in availability of options  with four can become a little too tight, and with two it's just blah.  With four the game changes significantly and there are less viable strategies, and it basically becomes who can place into souks first (i.e.- who gets the more beneficial rolls).  This I find can create a runaway leader problem, as the checks and balances afforded by  the quality of choices in a three player game  becomes significantly reduced in a four player game. i still like this game, but the drastic drop in it's appeal with any number other than the three hurts it's score.

UPDATE:  I find Yspahan to have very limited lasting power.  The game is ok, and can offer the occasional fun but it really never begs to jump off the shelf.

RATING: 8.7
  I like this because there are fairly interesting viable choices to be made on each turn.  Plays quickly, tactilely fun, blends luck/probability & strategy well, and includes multiple scoring tactics.

In others words, it can take me longer to teach it than play it, which can prejudice certain kinds of players before they sit for a game (ahem.. kids.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of the earliest games of this type, Yspahan's influence can be seen in later games like Kingsburg and Alien Frontiers. The dice are used to indicate which actions are available each round and how strong those actions will be. Because of this inherent randomness, strategy is difficult to execute, but tactical play is strongly rewarded. You need to be very adaptable.

After the dice are rolled, players will each pick an action to execute, with the starting player changing after everyone has gone. There is opportunity to stop other players, sometimes in very nasty ways, yet the player being picked on the most has a large advantage as their pieces get bumped to the caravan. It could even make sense to bump yourself there. Turns are very quick, as is the game as a whole, but what you do on your brief turn is often a difficult decision. And many of the choices you make can be further augmented by paying coins, complicating the decision.

While I prefer Alien Frontiers to this game, I'd be happy to play this one as well. It has very low rules overhead, yet presents players with a lot of choices each turn. Certainly a game worthy of the various accolades it has received.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Played it very few times, and am not fond of it because the luck element is hard to overcome with strategy.  Dice rolls are key -- if you need camels, and it's your turn to roll the dice, and you only get one lowest die, then you'll get only one camel (or two, if you have the right building), or choose to take one of the better groups of dice.  More than that, if you get the right cards (e.g. "get 3 camels", or "place a building with gold only", or "place a good on the caravan for free", or "place a shop anywhere on the board for free"), then you have a significant advantage that is hard to overcome.  I think getting the caravanserai and shipping goods for extra cards is a critically important strategy, but it can be overcome by bad luck for sure -- you need camels to make it work.

Overall, I'd rather play Goa, which has luck (need good expedition card draws), but is much richer and has several winning strategies.

RATING: 7



=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The core of Yspahan is simple to explain; a bunch of dice are rolled, then they are grouped by value and placed in the levels of a tower; the number of dice in the levels dictates how powerful the associated action will be.
There are 5 types of actions: taking gold, camels and placing cubes in sectors of Yspahan are all linked with a particular level of the tower, while moving the overseer and taking a card are things you can do whatever the level of the tower you select.
Placing cubes in the city will grant you victory points for completed sub-sectors (called souks), and sending goods with the overseer to the caravan will also grant points.
You can also have special powers with cards you draw and with some building you can build.

At the start of the week, a lucky roll for the first player has a quite huge impact, so I feel the luck factor is quite high; the dice you are left with on your turn dictate what you do mre or less effectively; of course you can decide to build stuff or to move the overseer, but the impact of these tactical choices is pretty close to nothing in comparison to the effetcs of a good dice roll.
I loved the game the first session we played it because I thought there were a lot of possibilities, so maybe I set my expectations too high; now, I am not that eager to play it. There are good ideas in Yspahan, but I feel that the dice system, while interesting, is at odds with the rest. It is a missed opportunity for a good game.

Component-wise, the game is very attractive; I liked the bright color scheme used, and the cameeples.
The rulebook could be more clear. Repetition of a few words here and there would have increased the rules clarity, and I even found a grammar error (I loathe that, especially when a spell checker would have found it); of course I am referring to the french rules, so this might not be true for your copy.

In conclusion, I think that Yspahan is original, but the luck factor was too high for me; it would have been better without the cards and the buildings, leaving no doubt about it's core nature: a light dice game.
Usually I find the production values of Ystari games to be near perfect, but this time there is clearly room for improving the rules.

RATING: 4
This was a disappointing design. It's like Yahtzee with a pasted on theme. Boring. Not enough challenging choices. I'm sure there's something to the game, but I just don't care. Not sure I was immersed enough in the theme to care. It reminded me of Roll Through the Ages, which is equally boring. Roll some dice, and make your selection--pick up camels, place some goods in some part of town, or pick up gold. In lieu of that, pick up a card, which gives you some advantages. The way the dice distribute the possible actions, and quantity available is novel, but that's about it. If I want to play Yahtzee, I'll play Yahtzee. Since I didn't care for Caylus, which is another Ystari design, I think I'll avoid their games in the future. Whatever. $49.95 retail?!? Too expensive for this light of a box. Even at $30 from Boards & Bits, this is too expensive for the bits, although there are plenty of wooden cubes and camels, but there's not much heft--there's something left to be desired.

Update (10/01/11): This found a loving home with someone who appreciates the design. I will probably never play it again, but you never know. Down to a '4'.

RATING: 9
One of my favorites.  On your turn, you roll dice, which you then group according to number, and then use to determine how many of a given action you can take.  It throws in two major scoring methods (souks and caravan) in addition to two minor methods (buildings and cards) to keep games from becoming stale, and presents the player with a surprising amount of control despite the dice rolling mechanic.  

Is it just me or does the artwork for all Ystari games slowly grow on you, until you come to view it as beautiful, despite an initial impression that sort of left you flat?

RATING: 7
First played August 21st, 2007.  This takes a bit to learn (~10min), however is a very quick play after the first play.  It took 3 of us about 45 minutes and the later rounds were wizing by, so no doubt it could be played in 30 min.  It seems to have a good balance of luck and strategy.  The dice rolling seemed to be a bit clunky, however we started helping each other out loading the dice on the board after a while and that seemed to go better.  Not sure how to make it better other than perhaps a compartment to roll the dice in so they don't go everywhere?  There seemed to be a few paths to take for victory as well.  Like most games, it will take a few rounds to get use to the mechanics and figure out a strategy to settle on.  Since plays fast and has many facets to the game, I would highly recommend giving this a gameplay and consideration in playing in your rotation of games on a regular basis.

RATING: 6
Yspahan is billed as a "dice game," but really the dice just dictate how powerful the actions to be chosen are.  The real game is in planning how to build your scoring engine.  Do you go for the caravan and hope for majority or purchase lots of buildings to dominate the city?  I like the split path to victory and the change in feel depending on the number of players as some strategies can become more or less valid.  It's a good, quick middle-weight game.

RATING: 7
initial rating 1 game 4 players

4 for 4 for Ystari, thats a lot of 4's and a lot of YS's too. 

Another really good game similiar to Mykerinos, not so much in actual mechanics but in the following ways:

 - Fast paced gameplay
 - plays in 30 - 45 minutes
 - Multiple paths to victory
 - 3 to 4 players but best with 4
 - Nice tough choices but no AP
 - Variable player powers
 - Slight screwage effect
 - About a mid-weight game

This had a really nice element of risk taking in that building late in the week could end up being a wasted action but it is still tempting for the points. Also, WHERE you choose to build is important as you could get jousted by the Supervisor (kinda like the Provost) and sent to the camel track, nice that this is not altogether bad though and sometimes you may even do it yourself to score points up there, especially if you have an incomplete neighborhood.

Lots of ways to score and lots of good choices throughout so luck really doesn't play much of a role like you might think. The dice really just determine which actions are up for grabs and I found it balanced out quite well with only slight variations. The one neighborhood did seem somewhat elusive as it needed the full spectrum from 1 - 6 thrown but then I think that was one of the main purposes for the "gold" dice that could be bought for 1 gold each; to add to the amount of dice thrown and therein increasing the odds for this and also for getting a nice 3 or 4 of a kind. Turn order was much more important actually and this changed every 'day/turn' so that was good too. Also, don't underestimate the Caravan track, some good points can be milked from there and building up 'your' board is quite important as well for the special bonuses, powers and points.

My only knock is it might be a little hard to stop the leader as breaking up his neighborhood will score him points in the Caravan and if he has camels his neighborhood stays intact on top of that. More plays will tell for sure and I'm looking forward to playing again as I am any game with some meat coming in under 45 mins.

[u]TIDBIT/VARIANT[/u] - To make the screwage a little more effective and possibly fix the runaway leader, adjust the Supervisor deferral cost. Instead of just one camel; 1 camel & 1 gold OR 1 camel & 2 gold OR maybe 2 camels. This will make it a little harder to avoid neighborhoods breaking up and also less attractive. If you really want to get 'nasty' allow placements in other people's incomplete neighborhoods, might be too vicious... Will try the first one soon and update...

RATING: 7.5
This game was fine. I liked the ability to distinguish yourself with the special power buildings, and I liked that there were different paths to follow. But it did seem like there were some strategies that relied more on dice rolls than others which is a bit unfortunate. And it often seemed like there was an obvious best choice in terms of which set of dice to take. This was at least partially because we kept getting rolls where we only saw three or four distinct numbers and so we weren't able to get into the more valuable areas, so that might have been a bit of an anomaly. There were definitely some interesting ideas here but there were enough kind of clunky aspects to keep this game from going beyond pretty good.

RATING: 6
This game feels like it wants to be deeper than it actually is. There are too many options to choose from given the length of the game. Do you try to maximize your neighborhood placements, move cubes to the caravan, try to get enough income to construct buildings, or go for the powerful action cards?

It seems like a "lite" version of Puerto Rico and Caylus mixed together to me. The PR part is in the points you get for shipping goods and constructing buildings with special powers. The Caylus part is the cube placement in the buildings and the moving of the supervisor.

I've only played two three-player games so far, but they were both won by the player who built all five buildings in the order they're listed on the building card. The caravan and action cards were used to a small degree in my first game, but I don't think they had much of an effect on the outcome. In my second game, the caravan and cards were completely ignored. Is the way we perceive the value of the caravan and cards flawed? Maybe. It still felt like I was  only getting to play half the game.

RATING: 8
Another in a long line of games where you have limited action selection, but with a twist... the actions available are determined by dice.  You NEVER have enough actions to do everything that you want to do... and there are only 21 turns in a game, so every action counts.  The fact that cards have multiple uses help immensely.

Don't underestimate the power of the caravan or your buildings.  Each provides additional victory points for you and, in this game, every point counts.  The margin of victory in almost every game I've played so far has been very close.  One game had the high and low scoring players separated by only 5 points.

Download the game from Westbank gamers... even at the 'easy' setting, you'll get a great AI to play with.

This is a neat little game that should appeal to the casual gamer and the more hardcore gamer.  It combines easy choices with medium strategy, so newcomers will probably lose their first game . . . but not by a lot.  I've introduced this game to my children with relative success, and I've hosted quite enjoyable game nights with this game.

I like Yspahan.  It's not T&E or Brass, but there's a lot going on here.  Yes, it uses dice, but it uses dice in a similar way to how TtR uses cards; that is to say, Yspahan uses dice in order to facilitate set collecting (the souks).  

One of the most beautiful boards I have.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The current starting player rolls a handful of dice and places these starting with the six and then low to high. Then the player selects one row that can give camels or coins, allow placement of cubes in the buildings on a particular section of the map, or allow movement of the pawn. It is then possible to buy buildings giving special abilities on your board using camels and coins. Obtaining all of a set of buildings on the map is required for scoring. Also the pawn lets you select a cube from the map to add to a separate bonus scoring track. Having a camel will preserve the cube at this point. Each player in turn does these actions based on that dice roll. Then player order changes and the next player rolls. The only weakness is that it often seemed like the obvious selection was to take the row with the most dice. Most of the strategy was in deciding when to buy buildings or using the bonus scoring. There is a certain fun factor in getting to roll lots of dice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Victory point race with a handful of ways to score and minor engine aspects. I'm really surprised the dice rolling and turn structure haven't dragged the BGG rating down more.

I've only played Yspahan against bots, but I'm rating it anyway! Bwaha! The computer game has its flaws (the interface is buggy, the 2-player game isn't a lot of fun, the 3-player game is too easily won, and the compulsory self-sacrificing kingmaking moves directed exclusively against the human player is a lazy and annoying way of doing AI programming), but kind of like Minesweeper it's a nice mindless thing to do while listening to the radio or something. Rating could go up if playing against people turns out to be quick and engaging, though I guess being deprived of camels for the first week and a half probably grates more when you're not going through the motions in front of the computer. 4-player high score: 116. (One of the bots has me beaten with 119, though. The one that's not on my left, if you catch my left-right binding drift.)

Update: Rating lowered one point for the compulsory self-sacrificing kingmaking moves.

RATING: 6

RATING: 9
Light and short, but excellent strategic economics game with a very original dice mechanism. Yspahan is one of those rare games with dice that I really like. Oh boy, there sure are a lot of dice, at least 9 each turn! But a variety of mechanics offers different ways to deal with the luck of the dice. Take the time to build up your economy during the first week and then start powering those victory point generators during the second and third week. Don't forget the caravan. There is definitely much more going on in this game than simply throwing dice and hoping for the best rolls. The pc version is ideal to learn the basic strategies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A really fun and neat twist on "dice game" combined with "card game" and board tactics.  Don't forget that little known rule (in the final section of the rulesbook) about having the option to discard a card to add a virtual die to your selected action.  I still favor the Hoist as probably my favorite buildings to have.  The two major strategies seem to be (1) acquiring Souks, and (2) casting off your cubes into the Caravan.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Yspahan is a very engaging "dice" game which has quite a few interesting choices to make for what is a generally a lighter game.  Dice will determine how many camels or gold can be taken, number of cubes to claim buildings in the souks, drawing of a card, or moving a supervisor.  Most times, the number of dice in the row dictate how many of the above you can take or place.  However, the supervisor moves the sum of the dice on the slot.  If you choose to draw a card, number of dice do NOT matter, you only draw one card.  Points can be scored by owning all the shops of a color in a souk, selling goods on the caravan, or through points earned by building enough special power buildings.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
3 plays with 3 players.

Nice, relatively light management game.  The components are nice.

The strategies are relatively obvious (the 3 x 2 things you can buy on your individual boards are grouped along each of the 3 basic strategies you can follow), and it is fun to try to make the one you have chosen work.  The way you make it work depend on the strategy chosen by the other players, which adds some replayability.  I regret that switching strategy is not so easy.

That said, it feels a little bit too thin on strategy and tactics for me to like it so much (compare Agricola,....).

RATING: 8
Another game in the long line of camel games (or to be more exact games that include camel pieces) which stretches across the desert as far as you can see.  Yspahan will however be towards the head of the caravan leading the way due to the closely fought nature of the game, the need to make the most of your options on each turn and the quick learning curve (I came last by a long way but in the third day I had the highest score of the round and I am sure if we played a game again I would probably still come last but not by so much).

The game is played fast, tension is sustained throughout, and the narrative of the game works well.  Credit to the designer and the Ystari team.

Your first impression may be that it seems over complicated or too many possible combinations but after the first round of turns everything would of clicked.  Like a camel it may appear awkward and seem to have parts where there shouldn't be but in reality this is a fast, efficient, well designed game which will see use for a long time in the future.

RATING: 8
This is a really good and tight game BUT you have to be careful with the first few games. The rules are easy to but there also easy to get wrong the first time you play!

In the first game we didn't know that when using the intendant to send a cube to caravan you can pay a camel to send a cube from your reserve. So the caravan wasn't a useful choice for ANYONE! It removed your cubes from your points scoring souks and using it to hinder you opponnent seemed to be a waste of an action.

In our second game we played with the right rules
and I scored 48 pts using the caravan! Couple with the Caravanserai (I got a slew of cards) this was a winning strategy. 

This game is really tight. Only 21 actions. You need to make each of them count. There is some opportunism in the game but usually if you plan well you can win. In case of doubt: dra a card! They are all useful and if at that time you don't need them they can be used to augment regular action. I find the camels to be REALLY important so on your turn try to snag them!

Update to 9 after 4 plays: it plays quick. Each turn is VERY interesting. You don't get a lot of turn so there is a lot of angst to what do you need to do to scrore points. You have to be opportunist, but I think this is more important to chose the dices you need (especially camel) even if you only get 1 or 2 dice. Building the "engine": Paddock   Caravanserai   Cube on caravan demand forward think and ask you to planify 3-4 turns in advance. A very good game.

RATING: N/A



***

A fun tactical game with a light veil of strategy that's accessible for all, and the gameplay is fast with little downtime. [b]Yspahan[/b] is the first euro I know of to employ dice in a truly unique way, and I think it is still unmatched. You've got a boatload of dice, a bunch of wooden camels, wooden gold coins, bunches of little colored wood cubes, action cards, souks, and caravans! It's a multiple area control game with some interesting twists. The game uses its theme well and the game components are fun with a lot of visual and tactile appeal. You're using the dice to create multiple choices for your turn, filling up the various souks to control them, and deciding when to send your guys (or your opponent's guys) to the caravans. There is a solid sens of game progression, and the decision points feel meaningful. The game does involve luck, but not as much as you'd think. 

We play it most often with two players, moving to a new day every time the dice changes players - this makes the game go super fast and fun. We don't change any of the other rules when playing with two people.

My family totally loves this game, so it gets played a lot. 

[Ystari-Rio Grande Games]

weight = 2.4

RATING: 7.5
Fun game.  Lots of ways to win, too much to do, too little time to do it.  There are dice, but the luck component is not that large.  The dice system makes it a more tactical game because depending on how the dice fall (for your or against you), you may be better off adjusting your overall strategy.  
Also, I think it works well as a 2 player game with each person playing 2 colors.  There is not a lot of direct interaction except possibly sending someone else to the caravan, just disallow that for your opposite color.

RATING: 7
A surprising game that did not look that impressive but has some interesting mechanics.  You roll dice and unless you roll all numbers not all areas are available for placement.  You have special cards you can use that provide in game benefits.  And you can kick a player out of a location and put them into caravans.  At the end of the game it can score for you.  It is a majority control/area control game with some minor "take that" mechanics.  I found it very fun, with interesting mechanisms but not very thematic.  Another gem in that old Ystari line.

RATING: N/A
1598. Yspahan the fair becomes the capital of the Persian empire. Thus, being placed at the center of the world, the city enjoys a period of cultural and economic blossoming. The cities and villages of the region intend to take advantage of this expansion. Caravans loaded with goods and jewels set out for the desert, bearing the promises of a radiant future....
The players embody merchants trading with Yspahan. Meaning to take advantage of the coming of the Shah’s supervisor, they score points by placing their merchandise in the right shops, by sending them to the caravan, and by constructing buildings.

RATING: 8
Wonderful dice game that manages to have lots of options and still remain fast & breezy. With all of those choices, this is more of a gamer's dice game than a family dice game, but in that respect it succeeds. You see people win with various strategies, which is always a good thing. To some extent you need to adopt your strategy to the way the dice roll, but less than in other dice games. You really can go into this one thinking you're going to pursue the caravan strategy, or the building/upgrade strategy, or the city strategy, etc.

RATING: 7
After seeing it on the shelves at my local game store back in 2006 or so, Yspahan bounced around in my head for four years before I picked up a copy -- something about it grabbed me. And I'm glad for that: This is an excellent, and surprisingly quick, game with a lot of interesting decisions.

The use of dice -- the tower -- is superb, and it drives a lot of fun choices and options while providing a balanced amount of chance. The end result is a fun game that's easy to pick up and which moves along briskly.

I do wish that instead of the three main boards, they'd just made one larger board that incorporated the tower and caravan; it's a bit fiddly as-is. Apart from that, the components are quite nice.

RATING: 5
[i]Yspahan[/i] is a game in which you are primarily focused on maximizing your own score as opposed to screwing others.  It is rarely if ever possible to block an opponent while advancing your own cause (exceptions to this are timing the scoring of the caravan and possibly sending someone to the caravan at the end of a round).  If you approach the game with this in mind, it is quite enjoyable.  It plays light and fast and it is always fun to roll a handful of dice.

RATING: 8.5
This is a lovely little game that is quick, but requires a lot of decision making. As dice games go, this is pretty much exemplary. Yes, there is an element of luck, but it is so well tempered by the 'tower' that there is a lot of tactical and strategic play. Works best as a 4-player, I think. With two, it's too easy to get gold and camels (build the cheap buildings), so you can easily populate the souks without worrying about the supervisor. Be warned: its large box suits its depth, but belies its short play time. First-timers may be left feeling a little short changed for the money they paid. All I can say is: keep playing. This game rewards repeated play.

RATING: 7
Initial rating:
Good game, better than expected. I've read all the reviews, and thought: "guess it's just another nice, entertaining, but average euro, I'll let this one pass by". But after sitting in on a session, I decided to buy it anyway. 

Why? Because it's actually better than the "average euro": it plays fast despite lots of options, there is enough player interaction, and it has a nice lot's-of-dice-but-not-so-much-luck mechanic that's feels new instead of a rip off (closest thing I recall is the dice mechanism used in Roma, and even that one isn't close)

Cons: 
It's pretty low on theme, combined with the  "various possibilities to gain VP's" that make it sound dull. So I still have to see how I rate it in a year. Sounds a bit like a good Knizia, doesn't it?

Having the same cubes for goods, "special houses" AND your personal point counters at least don't hide the "cube pushing" feel games like this have. So it's kinda honest in it's themelessness....

NL: Vlot spel, tactisch, niet te moeilijk en toch leuk.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
11-2007 A very enjoyable light/middle-weight euro.  It's basically an economic optimization / area-control game but with a somewhat unique dice mechanism (actually part of it may have been borrowed from Tonga Bonga) that drives the engine.  Players place their goods into buildings and will score if they can fill the entire building.  There is room for some nastiness (removing a player's goods from a building) but it takes up a precious action and moves that player's marker onto the caravan track which can also score points (a nice catch-up mechanism and a possible strategy since I believe you can eradicate yourself to the caravan).

9-10-2011 UPDATE: The game is losing some of it's appeal.  Knocked down rating 1/2 a point.  Still keeping it high for introducing such a great way to use dice.  Too bad Ystari's record has been somewhat dull since this one.

RATING: 7.5
An enjoyable area-control game wherein players attempt to control areas of a city.  The game has a unique mechanism for deciding where players can go; a single player rolls a set of dice, and then the dice are arranged on a board with six open spaces in sequential order.  The number of dice in each space determines how many moves into each area of the city a player can make (or how much gold, or other items, a player can get); this is a great mechanism for adding uncertainty into an area-control game, as what you can do on your turn is dictated by random chance (and your playing order).  This is relatively light as far as area-control games go (it's no El Grande), but there are enough choices that one must use tactics and strategy to win.  Definitely a keeper, though luck does play a large roll.

RATING: 7
8.5 after about 10 games using the very good PC version from West Park Gamers.  Yspahan is a good, quick and easy to learn game with interesting mechanics, different to most other games I've played.  I think it would make a good gateway game for non-gamers.  It reminds me a bit of blend of Yahtzee (the dice) Puerto Rico (the buildings and economic system) and Taj Mahal (focusing on a strategy).  The caravan strategy seems to be dominant but it weakens if more than one player focuses on it, which indicates inherently good balance in the game's design.  The outcome seems a bit too dependent on good luck with some early dice rolls when you are the first player, which prevents me from giving it a higher score. The PC version is a good way to try before you buy and/or practise your game, and its quite addictive! I've yet to try this with 2 or 3 players.
Revised to a 7.5. I still think that this is a fun game with a good application of lots of dice rolling, but there doesn't seem to be much that players can do to check the a player that gets a few good dice rolls early in the game. Being unlucky in a 10 minute computer game is no big issue, but being unlucky in a 45 minute board game agains 3 other players is not as appealing compared to other games. Probably for the same reason, there doesn't seem to be much tension in the game, although this might improve with more experienced opponents.

RATING: 10
Fantastic game. Good duration, good amount of thinking yet doesn't leave you burnt out. Very cool dice mechanism to determine the resources that enter the game. Cool interaction of mechanics where things can be used both in your favour and then used by others to work against you. Like the Supervisor and caravan, the rules for the cube placement into the souks or the buildings you choose to build.

The luck adds even more to this in my opinion. The dice can be lucky for you, delivering a very rewarding "Yes!" moment, then they can take that a way with an "Oh Sh*t!" moment. It's never something that leaves you feeling like you lost (or won) the whole game from the dice, maybe just took a wee hit on the way. Who wants to play a game where you can work out exactly how to win before you play anyway......

This is very addictive. There are strategies to try and different ways to win, the dice (and how others play) may just cause you to evolve from what you were doing to something new. Each game is different and I think this will add to the longevity of this game as a favourite.

Plays well with 2, using the official variant.

Very enjoyable with 4, different dynamics completely.

I think this game will get a lot of table time with my wife and I and our friends. This would be harder to explain to non-gamers.

The WestparkGamers PC version is great to play. Beats me almost every time and still I return for more floggings..... Such is Yspahan.

RATING: 7
Yspahan is a solid family style game. The dice action in the game gives the game a good crossover appeal to those gamers brought up on simple, luck filled games yet there is enough to think about that the Euro influences can clearly be felt. 

I do like the game as it plays quickly once you get into the flow. There is a bit of a thought though that the game is a bit simplistic... maybe that's not necessarily a bad thing in terms of the rule set and the game play itself but in terms of the actual strategic/tactical decision making there is a slight niggling feeling of "is that it?" sometimes. Depending on the roll of the dice the decisions are somewhat dictated to you by what quantity of dice are currently the most plentiful when it happens to be your turn. There certainly is some luck in the game but that can be dealt with to some extent by choices you make in trying to build up your weaknesses by playing to your strengths... a common feature in many Euro games... and one that can be felt in the mechanisms of Yspahan as well. 

All in all this isn't a game I would say is quite at the level of top tier games but it IS a good solid family oriented game playable really with almost all ages and for that it deserves a bit of extra credit. A solid 7 rating for now and that could probably climb a half point or so with further play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Can die rolls be done right in a game? Yspahan is the classic game frequently said to do this and quite successfully I must say. The rolled dice determine the actions available to the players and the strength of each action (the number of camels or gold that may be taken or the number of the shops that may be set up in the different neighborhoods). In addition, the way the dice are organized make more valuable neighborhoods less probable, adding yet another dimension to the decision space.

One potential concern is that the die rolls may make some actions clearly better than others, giving the player who happen to start that turn an unfair advantage by chance. On the other hand, small dice can still be used to send goods from shops to the caravan with a good return in victory points so perhaps this will even out. I may prefer Ystari Games' Amyitis (goods and caravans without dice) but Yspahan is a very good game too.

Update: Sold because I was worried that lucky die rolls when you happen to start a round may be too decisive.

RATING: 6
Yspahan packs an impressive amount of strategy into such a short playtime.  What I like most about it is that there are three distinct paths to victory, and the way those three paths interact in a metagame really adds a lot of depth.  In that sense, the game plays best with those who are familiar with it, but it is also a great game to introduce to new players.  The dice system is very cool, and the balance of everything is really interesting.

Yspahan has slipped slightly in my rankings, and thus has seen less plays, since the release of Notre Dame.  These two games fill very similar places in my mind, and Notre Dame being a little fresher and deeper seems to win out more often than not.  The biggest fault of Yspahan is its price.  For a small game, it's quite expensive.  I imagine the only reason it wasn't in one of the Ystari small boxes (Mykerinos size) was that the dice are an expensive component, and the big box was necessary for the price they had to charge.  Still, Yspahan is a very good game and I'm glad that I own it.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The active player rolls 9 dice, and the rolls are distributed along 6 rows. The active player chooses a row to execute some actions, then the next player chooses from the remaining dice to do an action. Actions are earn gold, place cubes in one of four areas, earn camels, move the supervisor or pick up a card. The number of cubes/gold/camels earned are the dice rolled on that row. Players can build up their player mat to earn VPs and give them special benefits. This is a very fun game, and you can pursue different strategies, depending on what you roll or are left with. I think it is one of the best games of 2006.

RATING: 6.5
A very good, innovative dice game, in which luck is a minor factor. The luck comes in the spread of the numbers, rather than the amounts rolled. The dice are used to create area dominance and gain resource (camels, money) that allow for the pursuit of other strategies or to supplement the area dominance. There are several ways to minimize the luck element (cards, special buildings, other game options like the supervisor). Most of the time the best player wins at this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Either a heavy dice game or a very luck-influenced tactical game, Yspahan is a quite interesting concoction: each day during three weeks a throw of a handful of dice dictates the amount of resources available for all players to make business (camels, money, shops to fill on the board). The dice are sorted by value into groups, and players have then got to choose the group that suits best either to pick up the resources, to pick up a card with a nice action, or to move an inspector who stops by a shop and sends the stone in this shop to the "caravans" (a score track). With gold and camels special buildings are built, which give privileges to the lucky player who bought them.

Even though the basic mechanism of the game is transparent, the management of the throws has got to be extremely skillful. Especially when the first throws don't give many camels or gold. This can be frustrating at some points, since there aren't enough throws to say that luck evens out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.4
Si basa sul lancio di dadi (un po' come Kingsburg), quindi con forte presenza della fortuna, malgrado ciò le dinamiche non sono male. La strategia verte sulla scelta dei quartieri da 'occupare' settimanalmente, ma si può scegliere di ignorare i quartieri privilegiando tessere benefit o arricchendosi con denari e cammelli. Un gioco carino, veloce, che però secondo me alla lunga risulta ripetitivo e monotono. Oltretutto il gioco presenta un bug che annulla ogni strategia e bellezza del gioco spingendo i giocatori a movimenti continui del pedone con troppi bonus derivanti dal 'terzo tabellone' di gioco. Evitate di giocare con giocatori che applicano questa strategia sistematicamente rovinando il gioco.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
An achingly difficult game with a diverting dice mechanic. There's an awful lot in Yspahan to keep track of, and the crucial not-enough-turns-to-do-what-I'd-like concept is cleverly melded into days and weeks. Personally, I can't see what it takes to actually be [i]good[/i] at Yspahan, but I'm willing to play a lot of it to find out.

[i]Traded for Dixit Odyssey in April 2012 UK Maths Trade[/i]

RATING: 4
[b]Yspahan[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (4):[/COLOR]

[i]After one play I was interested in playing again and had that opportunity this summer. Wow, was I disappointed. First off I was playing with some sharks at this game and never even had a chance, but I was honestly bored while playing. The game ended up feeling like a bunch of disjointed mechanics crammed to get her to make a game. There was very little excitement while taking your turn, none of the actions felt satisfying. The buildings powers are quite pedestrian and the area control relies on certain dice being rolled. I'm not someone that needs a strong theme in games, but at least make an effort. 

I've seen this recommended as a family game and couldn't disagree more. Just because a game can be easily learned doesn't mean it's a great family game. Yspahan is dry as toast and not a game I could see kids getting into. I don't have any interest in playing this again. [/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]I enjoyed my play of this game, but it is not a game I feel the need to seek out. Drafting from the dice pool was fun, but the choices you had to make seemed to be obvious most of the time.Most of the "difficult" decisions came when you were the one who rolled the dice and therefore got to choose first.

Overall I had fun, the game played quick and had enough light decisions to make it interesting. I think this would make a great, light family game that introduces some different game mechanics in a very easy to learn manner.[/i]

RATING: 7
規則:
*主圖板置於中央，駱駝商隊圖板與貨物塔置於兩旁，將功能卡洗好，時間方塊放置第一週第一天，白色小人放圖板中央，每人拿一方塊放0分位置
*每人拿一個顏色貨物方塊、建築圖板和2枚金幣，由最年長的玩家開始，順時針進行
*進行3個星期，每個星期有7天(回合)，每天遊戲分成2階段
1.擲骰 起始玩家擲9個白骰(每花一元可多擲一黃骰最多3)，骰完照數字分堆，從貨物塔最下面往上一堆一堆擺，最大數值的放在最上面那格(如果全部一樣都在最下面)
2.行動 由起始開始選擇一個行動移除對應該格所有骰子，起始玩家結束後就移除黃骰(黃色骰子只有起始玩家可使用)，之後每人做一次行動該天就結束，每格有三種行動可選
>領取同骰子數目的金幣、駱駝、或放貨物到對應市場，同顏色商店形成市集，玩家不能放到已有其他人放置的市集，且該區如果有自己未放滿的市集要優先放，一個商店一個方塊，沒商店可放就不能放
>移動市長 骰子點數的步數，可花金幣每一枚增減一步，移動時不能重複經過，停留位置的兩旁商片方塊放到駱駝區(順序由該玩家決定)，此時玩家也可棄一駱駝改從自己供應區出方塊
** 放到駱駝區要照順序左下開始，每隻一個方塊，馬上獲得下面分數，當全滿就記分之後清空，分數為總方塊數*該玩家最高分區的方塊
>抽一張卡片，輪到時如果沒骰可選就抽卡，卡片可在其他行動棄掉視為增加一顆骰數，或是使用卡片功能後棄掉
*骰子行動完後可建造建築物，支付費用放一個方塊視為建造根據第幾棟拿左邊分數
*一天結束，將貨物塔清空，時間軌上的「日」往前移一格，如果第7天結束，則該星期結束，起始左傳(小黑人)
*一星期的結束，每個填滿的市集拿上面分數，所有商店清空，駱駝區記分不清空，移動時間軌上的「週」，「日」移回1，並將市長移回十字路口，換起始
*遊戲結束 分數最高的玩家獲勝，如果平手則共享勝利。
**2人遊戲**                                                                                              
>遊戲時只使用8顆白色骰子，最後一天由最低分的玩家做為起始玩家，如果平手就照原本的遊戲的次序進行。
◎每天按起始對家起始次序行動(起始玩家2次，對家1次)，黃骰不移除，但只有起始能用，如果對家選該格就移除
*蓋建築物必須花費一次選骰行動
*駱駝商隊每行可以使用的駱駝從4隻變成2隻
**3人遊戲**                                                                                                                     
*駱駝商隊每行可以使用的駱駝從4隻變成3隻。
**4人遊戲**                                                                                              
*最後一天的回合改照分數低到高順序，如果有同分，由前一天較晚選骰的玩家先進行選骰。
**建築**
>拿駱駝多一隻
>拿錢多兩元
>移動白米寶可不花錢增減1~3步
>每送一個貨物到駱駝區抽一張卡
>星期結束 市集計分額外加兩分
>放方塊時多放一個

RATING: 8
An appealing implementation of dice, allowing them to convert into action points for building to different sections of the board, with fallback options available. The action cards provide interesting options and bonus points, and one of the attractions of the game revolve around deciding when action cards are primary options and when they're fallback options. Add a strategic option to forsake buildings by investing in the caravan, which can multi-score, and you have a lot of things to manage and decide within a 30 minute game, with dashings of luck to spice things along.

RATING: 8
Yspahan almost always feels like it's over too soon. It can be difficult to find a good strategy in such a short amount of time, but it also seems like there are at least a few different paths to victory. Some players will be fans of the camel track and others will prefer to place cubes on the main board, or you can have a mix of both. The dice have a very small chance of messing things up, especially since players will be rewarded with cards if they can't complete any other action. The cards are perhaps the most random aspect of the game, definitely only useful on a case by case basis.

Overall: Yspahan isn't perfect, but it is simple, fast and elegant. A worthy addition to my collection.

RATING: 6.5
An interesting points scoring game. Players roll dice, which determine the available actions then take actions in turn from them, over 21 turns. Every 7 turns players score points, some one-off, and some that score in each scoring phase.
It is fairly easy to learn, though there are some bitty but important rules it is easy to forget. There is a nice balance of luck nd skill and it plays quickly, but there probably isn't very much depth, as certain combos are just better than others.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Yspahan is a euro of the days of old with few moving parts and a cohesive structure, and one where randomness can be mitigated enough to not sour your disposition. Most of my plays were the base game, and only recently with the expansion that seeks to balance the strength of the caravan. I would venture to say that there is no reason to ever play without the expansion (if you own it) as it adds some new cards and new ways to utilize your cubes. One card I like in particular allows you to take (what you deem) un-usable cubes and convert them to resources, which was a significant boon at the right time. The game does not feature ~heavy~ player interaction, per se, but the central board, the caravan, and surveyor mechanisms all play into a semi-interactive feel. It is not in-your-face, but it is certainly there, and you are vying for control of the board all the same.

This is a game that strikes a balance between filler and lightweight game: interesting enough to give seasoned gamers some crunchy decisions and fanciful enough to draw newbies in. Overall, my plays of Yspahan were sharp and delightful romps that I quite enjoyed.

(+) Quick playing, smooth turns with cohesive mechanisms.
(+) Varying amount of strategies to pursue.
(+) Randomness can be mitigated (discard a card to add a dice action, pay gold to have better choices, or use card effects to supplement your turn).

(-) The gameplay can feel like you are coerced to investing in others' strategies to keep up with them (i.e. the Caravan). Although this is notably turned down in the expansion, if only a handful of people monopolize the caravan, the difference in scores will clearly show it.
(-) The disadvantage of being last is felt more strongly in this title than some others, as your best choices are few and far between.
(-) It may lack longevity/depth.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
