RATING: 8
À la première partie, j'étais dubitatif. Plein de dés, des petits cubes en bois colorés qu'on pose un peu partout sur un décor pastel fort peu à mon gout. Et puis je perds de quelques points. Bref, une approche moyenne pour un jeu à première vue sans prétention.

Trois semaines plus tard, nouvelle partie… Tiens, ça a l'air pas mal cette caravane… Mais pour la rentabiliser, il faut quelques cubes par ci, un batiment construit par là… Et on est incroyablement pressé par le temps qui court à une vitesse folle. Tant que la partie s'est terminée sans avoir vu le temps passer.

Après une dizaine de parties, je n'ai plus que les couleurs à critiquer : le jeu est admirablement fluide, foncièrement original dans l'utilisation des dés et la dualité ville/caravane, parfaitement équilibré notamment dans le dosage du hasard à la Colovini, et je pourrais continuer à aligner les  superlatifs. Un TT d'or mérité, et un SdJ ou un DSP serait tout le mal qu'on pourrait souhaiter à Yspahan.

Il semblerait cependant que la dimension stratégique d'Yspahan ait souffert d'un grand nombre de parties, notamment sur Internet, et une stratégie « gagnante » semble aujourd'hui toute tracée : viser cartes et chameaux en première semaine pour construire rapidement le Caravansérail, se positionner au centre dès le début de la deuxième semaine pour pouvoir envoyer un grand nombre de chameaux à la caravane.

Dès lors, tout le jeu se situe dans la réaction au jeu adverse (remplir la caravane avant la fin de la deuxième semaine, jouer le Marchand au moment opportun…), profiter des opportunités (gros tirages sur le quartier des vases ou du coffre) pour faire la différence, notamment également dans la construction des bâtiments.

C'est déjà pas mal, mais c'est un peu dommage.

RATING: 7.8
Yspahan -- Played four-player.  

Poor luck is mainly balanced by the various choices, but one player's [i]good[/i] luck can sometimes be hard for the other players to overcome.  If that player benfits more than once, you're looking at a runaway leader.  Players must remain aware of this and be willing to sacrifice some opportunities to keep this in check.  

I generally avoid conflict, but I find that Yspahan does not provide enough opportunity for players to reduce one anothers' scores.  For example, a dice-roll may result in several of one number and very few of the others.  The first player to choose will greatly benefit, so the others need to be willing to use their actions to counterbalance that benefit -- Sadly, moving the supervisor [b]may[/b] subtract some points (if the first player has no camels [i][b]and[/b][/i] no chance to later replace the token), but it returns no less than 3 points to that player, and potentially more points if the caravan later lengthens.  A token placed in the caravan's first row in the first week will score by game end no less than 5 points (2 for placement, 1 for each of 3 weeks), but if that player gets any tokens in higher rows, the first token will be worth even more.  A shrewd player will keep camels handy, so the supervisor poses no real threat to him.  This means players can't afford to wait to see if good luck will strike the same player twice.  Players must aggressively take advantage of any opportunities to reduce his bounty.  

I found this game quite fun, but I find myself hoping for an expansion or variant which will counteract the impact of good luck.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
After a year of so of my first play - I soloed it. It's not that bad, but I had justified reasons to hate it. And I'm sure I've seen some rules the first time (ahem). 

- It's a MPS not unlike worker placements (I'll argue it is one, only you're taking dice instead of placing - in any case it's another take on action selection). 
- Like many MPS games it's got preprogrammed paths to victory and like many MPS games of its era there's no synergy between them, no theme that would function as user interface - the way one is supposed to actually use all these arbitrary VP distribution tables is opaque and clunky .  
- Another thing I dislike (hate?) is demand to build an engine, but giving you very little actions in which to do so (yay optimisation!). You really must know the game well to know how to jungle this idiocy, I say idiocy because learning this is neither fun nor stimulating.
- Art. I seriously dislike old ystari art. First there's no theme worth naming such, then the art is kinda static and ...dead. Drenched in pastels to not be as obvious. 

What suprises me that people rate this higher than Knizia's Marco Polo - okay so that one is just set collection/hand management/Knizia's game of chicken type of game, yet theme makes much more sense. And has camels! This is just a light version of VP spread sheet matrix for those who want to calculate these sort of games - and has dice for randomness, woo hoo. 

EDIT - the main reason this has fallen to 1.0 is a really depressing visual style. And pretty dull and boring gameplay. 

[hr]
ADDENDUM (years later)

Oh dear. I had to read my comment after playing it for the last time as I really suppressed the memory of it existing. (It's a 1.0) I have no idea what's with the Ystari line, especially the older games. I can't imagine how uglier could they be. You might think I hate brown, well, yeah, except for chocolate and clothing (I do wear brown), but in clothing I seriously loathe speckled gray, the stalest colour one could possibly wear. Ystari line pretty much looks like pastelish and brownish and speckled had a child, the child always came in the graphic design style at least 10 years older than the game in question.
As for the game itself, my notes say it's an action selection full of arbitrary parts you must optimise around its VP spread sheet martric and it has no theme nor rhyme nor reason, ergo it's dull as hell. My notes don't actually say the last thing, but it's hinted in the sarcasm.

RATING: 7
I've only played this game against the solidly implemented AI, and I often struggle against it. In this game, players roll a whole lot of dice and group them to certain actions according to the number of pips. Then players take turns choosing which action to take.

Luck plays an important role here as it often comes down to the luck of the draw. Getting camels, especially early in the game, seems to be a huge advantage. Fun, but occassionally frustrating when rolls don't go your way more than once.

RATING: 8
A few people I know dislike this game due to their concerns over the randomness of dice. The more I play this game, the more apparent it becomes that the dice provide a decent challenge to your progress and decision making. Players who always choose which ever section has the most dice won't necessarily win. The key to winning is made up of good decisions and timing. Obtaining cards is a strong advantage and the caravan is a focal point for extra score. I encourage people to play this game at least three times before dismissing it for the dice mechanic.

RATING: 9
Wheee!  There is something about this game that is just all out fun.  I think it is because you are rolling a ton of dice, but you are never short on great options.  The game chops along nicely, in fact so quickly that half the time you feel you are up against the wall trying to get everything done that you'd like.  

There is a really wonderful balance to the game.  The Caravan strategy (yes, I've won going heavy in the cavavan), and the building strategy both are viable.  I like how the cards aren't too powerful, but are worth just enough to increase your desire to get them.  There is a lot of game packed into such a short length.

RATING: 9
I like the multiple ways of scoring, the fast pace of the game, and the fact that you have enough time to really fire all your guns, in terms of scoring. The seven turns between scoring rounds really gives you an opportunity to set yourself up on multiple fronts, while the random die rolls dictate what actions are available. There are precious few die rolls that result in no workable option, and taking a card is not really a bad thing.

I'm getting a little tired of the design paradigm that says that you must be starving at the banquet of possible actions. Hell, let my opponent and I take the actions that we want to take, and let's see who did better! It's the first Euro that I've been really excited about for awhile.

RATING: 7
One of the first "hey this is a cool new way to use dice" dice games! I really liked it, but cooled on the game a bit after a while when the caravan strategy -- send your cubes to the caravan whenever possible -- seemed overwhelmingly strong.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
At the start of each round, the starting player rolls a bunch of dice (possibly with extra dice available only to the first player, at a cost). These d6 are allocated to action spaces according to their die face. Each space allows you to either place markers in different regions of the board, earn currency (gold or camels), get a special card, or move markers to the caravan (for VP scoring later). You can also buy a building on your player board, which improves the other actions for you in some way. The cards can be played for various bonus effects, too.

At the end of 7 rounds (days), score (and clear) each board area; also score the caravan (more markers = more VP), which is NOT cleared until it is actually completely filled. At the end of 21 rounds (3 weeks), the player with the most points wins.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One of the best new games I have played in a while.  The use of the dice for selecting actions produces just the right amount of randomness to keep the game from becoming too dry.  It's easy to learn, plays quickly, and presents some tantalizing options on each turn.  Update:  can't get enough of it!  Contemplating giving out my first ten in a while.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dice rolling Euro with multiple ways to score victory points. Each turn nine dice are rolled and these are placed depending on the number they show. Once the dice have been placed they can be used to perform actions whether it be acquiring camels or gold, placing goods in the shops within souks, or transferring goods to the caravan. Points are obtained by having goods in all the shops of a certain souk, having built a certain number of buildings (which in turn provide added bonuses) or having a certain number of goods in the caravan. There are quite a few options available each turn and the most obvious one isn't always the best, plus games tend to be quite tight with it being difficult for a runaway leader to emerge. Overall 'Yspahan' is a good well-balanced game but is probably slightly too dependent on the luck of the dice rolls to determine a winner preventing it from entering the upper echelons of this type of game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played 1+ times.  There appears to be multiple paths to victory and the action selection mechanic with the dice is quite refreshing and neat.  The game can definitely be affected by good and poor roles, but I think it's robust enough to reward skillful play most of the time. 2008/10/08

RATING: 6
This game is really ahead of its time, and I am also not at all surprised to see that it was released right around the time Caylus came out. The point conversion and cube placement mechanics will seem vaguely familiar to a worker placement fan even if they are only tangentially related.

Using dice for resources is interesting because you never quite know what you will get. You can make plans, but the roll of the dice can force you to alter those plans. While this makes the game more tactical, there is still room for long term planning. I also enjoy how all of the players must make a choice based on the same dice roll. This adds an extra layer of maneuvering because you have to worry not just about your own plans, but also those of the competition.

There are definitely a handful of set strategies in the game. Some people may enjoy this aspect of the game. I find it somewhat limiting, but I would have to play the game more to know whether these strategies are flexible or rigid. I should also point out that this is not an uncommon criticism of mine for a variety of games, not just Yspahan. Finally, the strength of the buildings seems obvious much in the same way that getting more dice in Roll Through the Ages or Alien Frontiers is very powerful. Again, I need more plays to know for sure.

Overall, this is a good game with lots to think about on each turn. I look forward to repeat plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is an interesting game that has a few things in common with Caylus, and yet is pretty different.  The big dice rolls at the beginning of each turn can really vary.  You want a lot of a kind, or you kind of blow the first turn advantage.  There are a number of ways to score points, and balancing everything makes for a fun game.

RATING: 10

Update: Yspahan still hits a sweet spot and hits the table often three years after being published. Upgraged to the elite ranks of the very few games rated a "10" in my collection. Note: I still rate Yspahan a 10, but only as a three-player game, where it really shines. With four players, balance issues emerge and the fourth player is, in my experience, at a pretty big disadvantage.

RATING: 7.5
Rating could move up or down here.  I have had a lot of fun with this on my first four plays.  I love dice and I love games that use dice in interesting ways.  

The real beauty of this is that, once players have some experience, it plays quite quickly.  It truly belongs in the category of super-filler; plays in under 45 minutes but has some real depth.  Other games like this are Hansa and Mykerinos.  Yes the players have to have played a few times, but they don't have to be veterans. 

My wife and I can play Notre Dame in about a half-hour, but this is a function of just being two players and our having played over 20 times, so I don't categorize it as a true super-filler. 




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 9
Yspahan has lovely components in vibrant colors. The game is a clever mechanisms of choices. There are 12 dice in this game and almost no luck. Cudos to the designers for such a refreshing mechanism. The game is all about choices and in a classic Ystari tradition the scoring mechanisms allow for a variety of different strategies. It plays fast, we play with 4 "weeks" sometimes (28 turns) instead of 3 "weeks" (21 turns). No idea why the game should stop at 3 weeks (it feels short, where 4 weeks feel just right). The only thing that the game lacks is more conflict between players. Somehow there are not many ways to "punish" your opponents. Hint: You need to build buildings as soon as possible and stick to a strategy. Get the new cards from the Ystari treasure box, they add so much to the game!

RATING: 6
08/2008:5
Game has a unique twist with the dice roll. I find the dice gives the game an additional luck factor which upon multiple plays on the computer, becomes annoying. clearly, it is more favorable to roll similar numbers during your turn, even though it may not exactly be the thing you are looking for. I like the game, but not quite sure how much I enjoy the luck aspect of the game.On subsequent plays, I think the dice rolls is just way too unpredictable and also influences the outcome way too much. I love luck in games, but I do not like luck taking over the entire game. This game makes "choices" rather limited.

072811 Replaying this game after several years have reconfirmed my fears that the luck of the roll is quite heavy. In a four player game, each person gets to roll 5 times, perhaps 6 if you come in last during the final round. Getting to choose camels and money is critical during your roll because if the camel and gold options are even remotely enticing during other players turn, they will be gone by the time they reach you. So, if you roll poorly and the dice are evenly distributed during your turn, it is hard to play catch up. Having opted for a more focused caravan strategy, I am not sure it alone is sufficient for victory. Perhaps one must start really early, in the first round. But, unlike other games where the strategy is to go for actions that no one else is taking, the caravan strategy is valuable only if others are willing to at least chip in to increase the multiplier. Overall a decent game, but too much luck. The luck aspect of the dice rolls are too hard to overcome given the low amount of dice rolls per player.

RATING: 8.5
This was one of my favorites from BGG.con, although I missed out on some notable possible contenders.  I got a little worried when I saw the hugs pile-o-dice, but once it was explained how they work, I relaxed.  Sure, there is a big random element to the game, but I think it works very well.  

The die are allocated to action slots on a side board.  Every die of the lowest number goes in the bottom slot (Camels) and the highest valued die on the top (Gold).  Each remaining set of numbers goes on slots for the four neighborhoods, filling the bottom first.  The actions are simple.  Take camels in the lowest slot, allocate people (cubes) to stores in the neighborhoods, or take gold in the highest slot.  Once you take your action, the next player chooses from what's left and so-on.  Since you can only take an action where there are die available, the most valuable neighborhood is frequently unavailable.

There are some additional actions that are always available.  One is to move the overseer by a number of spaces on the die you choose (this is the only time that the value of the die affects your actions).  If there is a cube in a store next to the space he lands on, that cube is sent to the caravan.  This can be a bad thing for a player because it might break up a complete set of stores (only complete sets score), or it can be a good thing because you get score in the caravan as well, and if you have a cube in an incomplete neighborhood, you might as well send him to the caravan.

The other special action is to take a card.  These cards can be played at any time and they typically get you goods, extra placements, or let you build things for less.

Speaking of building, the last major aspect of the game is the building board.  There are 6 buildings on the board and each one gives you a nice advantage.  When these buildings are active, you can some very nice bonuses.  I cleaned up in my first game by building more than anyone else.  I definitely didn't get away with that twice!

One of my favorite things about the game is the pace.  Like Mykerinos, which I also really like, it maintains a brisk, but tense pace through-out.  I always love a game that takes 45 minutes and leaves you feeling like it wasn't just a time-killer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I liked this game the first couple times I played it.  Since then, the caravan strategy has a tendency to dominate the game.  Pair placements on the caravan with the caravanserai building, which allows you to take a card every time you put a cube on the caravan, and you'll frequently coast to an easy victory.  Being able to score cubes on the caravan twice or more given the end of the week scoring is strong enough, but being able to take a card every time you put a guy on the caravan is ludicrously good.  The strategy can become stronger still if the caravan player also manages to get the buildings to move the supervisor more flexibly and the building to get more camels.  In order to be competitive with somebody running a caravan strategy, you basically have to run a caravan strategy yourself.  

This game has the potential to be a nice short-but-meaty affair, but I think that it would take some house-ruling, and I don't want to have to find house rules for a 45 minute game.  There's other games that fit into that niche that don't require balancing.

RATING: N/A
1598. Yspahan the fair becomes the capital of the Persian empire. Thus, being placed at the center of the world, the city enjoys a period of cultural and economic blossoming. The cities and villages of the region intend to take advantage of this expansion. Caravans loaded with goods and jewels set out for the desert, bearing the promises of a radiant future...

The players embody merchants trading with Yspahan. Meaning to take advantage of the coming of the Shah’s supervisor, they score points by placing their merchandise in the right shops, by sending them to the caravan, and by constructing buildings.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I like the dice-rolling mechanism in the game, and I like the different choices you have between ways you can score points.  We never moved the supervisor in our one playing of the game, because we never could see how it could be advantageous to do so, and after several games against the computer, I don't see that it's used that often.  It MAY fall apart if someone takes the power card route, but really, you can't take JUST the caravan route, or JUST the majorities route, or JUST the power cards route to win the game.  You really have to balance it all, and it's a novel use of dice to eliminate much of the luck.  I also wonder if the player who gets first pick early in the game has an advantage; in some games against the computer, having a great die roll on the first turn, when going first, can almost ensure that you will win.  Regardless, the short play time of this one will ensure a permanent spot in my collection.

100-plus plays: computer
1 play: 3 players

First games left a "that's it?" feeling; that passed and this has grown on me. It's got just enough under the hood, and yet zips along.  I do think more cards would be nice, and the 3-camel card is clearly too strong (with the "2 pts per camel" card bringing up the rear).  So perhaps a rough edge or two, but clever and fun. Reminds me of Roma in that way: so much to like, but not quite polished.  Also, my wife likes it a lot, which is a huge boost for any game under my roof.

I must say, though, $50 is WAY too much for this really rather small game.  Must be the dollar/euro thing....

RATING: 7.5

RATING: 9
I picked this up in part because I already had the "Souks" expansion in the Ystari box. It's a great game, and quickly became one of my favourites.

The layout with 3 boards (plus one per player) is as unusual as the dice-based set-matching gameplay. And I love the little wooden camels! The different paths to victory provide a complexity that belies the speed it plays at.

Upgraded rating to 10 as of 12/09/12. Downgraded back to a 9 as of 01/08/13. After almost 20 plays I'm more aware of its limitations. Still a great game, though!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Truthfully I have avoided Yspahan for a long time.  Even though the use of the dice is clever, I was still turned off by the reliance of them.  I'm happy to report that my disdain for the mechanic is somewhat unfounded, as there are plenty of decisions beyond the simple die rolling.  However, not all of those choices were created equal.  For example, the caravan becomes relatively useless if no one else gets in on the action.  Gold and camels are of lesser importance as the game progresses once you have constructed most of the buildings.  I was able to score more than half of my total points just by controlling the board in the last week.  I can see why some people have claimed to have figured this game out, and it would be a fatal flaw if Yspahan does not offer enough variety in strategy after successive plays.  Even so, for now, I'm fairly hopeful and would not mind playing again.

Update: So I finally got to witness firsthand how you can abuse the caravan track to win this game by a landslide.  The strategy is perfectly sound and legal, but it is just so completely broken.  Therefore, I reduced my rating for Yspahan and have decidedly put this game in the "will not likely to play again" category.  It is quite disappointing to find out that there exists one way of playing which trumps all others, kind of makes me wonder what's the point then?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.6
When I saw this game last Saturday at Spel 2011 in Broechem, Belgium I was hearing that sentence over and over again in my head. “All Ystari games are great….. All Ystari games are great…. All Ystari games…..”. The next thing on my mind was “Camels, this is the camel game!”.

Those two combined were the reason we bought this game. If we should have picked it from the look of the box, we won’t have bought it probably. This was the Master Print Edition. Don’t know what the difference is with a regular edition (except of the box art). We couldn’t wait to play this one, but we did it also. Two long days we waited. Watched a review yesterday, and started playing. The review we watched (in Dutch), was great. We didn’t have to read any rules (except the part for the 2 player changes), and just start playing.

To be honest, I expected more of this game. I think it’s because of the name Ystari on the box, I was expecting a more strategic game than that it is. Nevertheless, it’s fun. Maybe a bit too much luck involved  with all the dice. If you have bad luck, you throw a lot of the same numbers, and that way, there aren’t too many options too choose from. But as said, it’s fun, and we may play it regularly. This is a game I would recommend to other people.

RATING: 6
A bit middle-of-the-road when it comes to Eurogames, this game actually has quite a few things working against it: A boring, overused theme; only playable by 3 or 4 players; a lot of little rules to learn for a game that only lasts 30-40 minutes.

The experience itself is engaging, and some of the mechanics feel fresh, but there a plenty of parts that I'd do away with, or re-design. The dice allocation is neat, but the caravan is perfectly boring and its ideas have been seen before. The cards also needed a bit more spice. 

This is a game I enjoy playing, but it isn't hard to find something a bit more engaging these days.
